<b>b</b> density of LINE transposons, <b>c</b> density of SINE transposons, <b>d</b> density of DNA transposons, <b>e</b> gene density, <b>f</b> gene expression levels, <b>g</b> <i>Ka/Ks</i> of syntenic gene pairs identified between chrX.1 and one of chrX.2, chrX.3, chrX.4, <b>h</b> <i>Ka/Ks</i> of syntenic gene pairs identified between chrX.2 and one of chrX.1, chrX.3, chrX.4, <b>i</b> <i>Ka/Ks</i> of syntenic gene pairs identified between chrX.3 and one of chrX.1, chrX.2, chrX.4, and <b>j</b> <i>Ka/Ks</i> of syntenic gene pairs identified between chrX.4 and one of chrX.1, chrX.2, chrX.3. Links in the core connect synteny blocks, blue ribbons indicate synteny blocks between chrX.1 and chrX.2, chrX.3, chrX.4, green ribbons indicate synteny blocks between chrX.2 and chrX.3, chrX.4, red ribbons indicate synteny blocks between chrX.3 and chrX.4. Source data are provided as a Source data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Assembly, similarity, and divergence of allelic chromosomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Assembly, similarity, and divergence of allelic chromosomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-16338-x/MediaObjects/41467_2020_16338_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-16338-x/MediaObjects/41467_2020_16338_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="443" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b> Overview of Hi-C heatmap for assembled chromosomes. Each allelic group contains four chromosomes, there are few linkages between allelic groups, indicating high quality chromosome-level scaffolding. <b>b</b> Chr1.1 and chr1.2 were chosen as examples to illustrate the assembly quality. The Hi-C heatmap shows the contiguity within chromosomes and the linkage between allelic chromosomes, the depth shows the even coverage of assembled sequence. The syntenic blocks show good synteny between chr1.1 and chr1.2, and two inversions were detected by both Hi-C heatmapping (red rectangles) and synteny (red ribbons). <b>c</b> Frequencies of synonymous distances between syntenic gene pairs, cross-comparing <i>M. sativa</i> (Msa) and <i>M. truncatula</i> (Mtr).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We found a low level of sequence divergence (~0.01) between any allelic chromosome pairs and the genetic linkage map could not distinguish homologous relationships (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">5</a>), suggesting they have experienced abundant recombination, providing the molecular evidence for the conjecture that the cultivated alfalfa is a tetrasomic inherited autoploid plant in which bivalent pairing is random and not preferential<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Dilkova, M. &amp; Bingham, E. Microsporogenesis of alfalfa cultivars and selected genotypes II. Medicago Genet. Rep. 17, 1–16 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d51675e1094">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Stanford, E. H. Tetrasomic inheritance in alfalfa. Agron. J. 43, 222–225 (1951)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d51675e1097">24</a></sup>. Therefore, it is conceivable that the four allelic chromosomes are mostly functionally equivalent, like the two allelic chromosomes in diploid species. To validate this hypothesis, we compared the four allelic chromosomes systematically. The results showed that the four allelic chromosomes were highly similar in terms of size, number of genes and contents of repeat elements (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM6">2</a>). Plots of the synteny relationship and <i>Ka/Ks</i> ratio of each syntenic gene pair, clearly show a high degree of conserved synteny, with no substantial overall <i>Ka/Ks</i> ratio difference between any two allelic chromosomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#Fig1">1</a>). We also investigated expression levels of genes in each allelic chromosome group, and detected no significant overall allele dominance in the expression profiles of the cultivated alfalfa (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">7</a>). All these results indicate that the autotetraploid alfalfa is a stable, random pairings autotetraploid species, unlike the more common situation of returning to diploid state accompanied with massive gene loss after whole genome duplication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Julier, B. et al. Construction of two genetic linkage maps in cultivated tetraploid alfalfa (Medicago sativa) using microsatellite and AFLP markers. BMC Plant Biol. 3, 9 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d51675e1117">25</a></sup>. This situation has hindered deciphering genome all the time. Fortunately, based on the most optimal technology available to date (accurate CCS reads, Hi-C data, and allele-aware assembly algorithm), we successfully assembled all allelic chromosomes for one plant of cultivated alfalfa, although there may exist some errors in phasing the four allelic chromosomes due to its essential features of tetrasomic inheritance. In addition, we have to point out that to correctly phase four homologous chromosomes is only meaningful for individual plant in such tetrasomic and self-incompatible alfalfa, as the widespread recombination occurs in various cultivars and different individuals. Nevertheless, this well-organized chromosome-level assembly has sufficient quality for most genetic dissection and breeding research on cultivated alfalfa.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Whole genome duplication and bursts of transposable element</h3><p>We next inferred the phylogeny position and divergence times between cultivated alfalfa and another ten legume species and grape (<i>Vitis vinifera</i>). Here we selected the first group (chr1.1–chr1.8) to represent monoploid alfalfa in this analysis. In total, 569 single copy genes were identified and used to construct the phylogenetic relationships, via the concatenated and multispecies coalescent approach (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">8</a>). The results indicate that cultivated alfalfa and <i>M. truncatula</i>, the most closely related species, diverged ~5.3 (3.7–7.3) million years ago (Mya). As complex polyploidization events occurred in ancestral or specific legume species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Cannon, S. B. et al. Multiple polyploidy events in the early radiation of nodulating and nonnodulating legumes. Mol. Biol. Evol. 32, 193–210 (2015)." href="#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d51675e1138">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wang, J. et al. Hierarchically aligning 10 legume genomes establishes a family-level genomics platform. Plant Physiol. 174, 284–300 (2017)." href="#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d51675e1138_1">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178–183 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d51675e1141">28</a></sup>, we also used 980 conserved BUSCO genes and 5305 low copy genes (≤10 genes for each species) to infer the phylogeny, resulting in the same topology as that obtained using single copy genes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">9</a>). All the phylogeny analyses provided very high support (bootstrap values equal to 100% or posterior probability equal to 1) for each node, except the basal lineages <i>Arachis</i> and <i>Lupinus</i>. This may be due to the very recent divergence (~4.58 Mya) of the ancestral legume into <i>Arachis</i> and <i>Lupinus</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">8</a>), and incomplete lineage sorting and early gene flow may have influenced the robustness of this topology<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Yang, Y. et al. Prickly waterlily and rigid hornwort genomes shed light on early angiosperm evolution. Nat. Plants 6, 215–222 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d51675e1164">29</a></sup>.</p><p>The haploid size of the cultivated alfalfa genome (assembled 2738 Mb/4 = 685 Mb) is 295 Mb greater than that of the <i>M. truncatula</i> genome (published assembly is 390 Mb although the estimated size was 430 Mb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Young, N. D. et al. The Medicago genome provides insight into the evolution of rhizobial symbioses. Nature 480, 520–524 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d51675e1174">12</a></sup>). We analyzed whole-genome duplication (WGD) events and the transposable element (TE) content in these allelic chromosomes, which have had profound effects on plant genome evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Wendel, J. F., Jackson, S. A., Meyers, B. C. &amp; Wing, R. A. Evolution of plant genome architecture. Genome Biol. 17, 37 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d51675e1178">30</a></sup>. Distributions of synonymous substitutions per synonymous site (<i>Ks</i>) within genes in syntenic blocks clearly indicated that the same ancient WGD occurred in the evolutionary history of all 11 genome-sequenced legume species (<i>Arachis duranensis</i>, <i>Cajanus cajan</i>, <i>Cicer arietinum</i>, <i>Glycine max</i>, <i>Lotus japonicus</i>, <i>Lupinus angustifolius</i>, <i>M. sativa</i>, <i>M. truncatula</i>, <i>Phaseolus vulgaris</i>, <i>Trifolium pretense</i> and <i>Vigna angularis</i>) compared in this study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Pfeil, B. E., Schlueter, J. A., Shoemaker, R. C. &amp; Doyle, J. J. Placing paleopolyploidy in relation to taxon divergence: a phylogenetic analysis in legumes using 39 gene families. Syst. Biol. 54, 441–454 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d51675e1220">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Cannon, S. B. et al. Polyploidy did not predate the evolution of nodulation in all legumes. PLoS ONE 5, e11630 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d51675e1223">32</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#Fig2">2c</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">10</a>). The estimated date of this WGD (~58 Mya)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Pfeil, B. E., Schlueter, J. A., Shoemaker, R. C. &amp; Doyle, J. J. Placing paleopolyploidy in relation to taxon divergence: a phylogenetic analysis in legumes using 39 gene families. Syst. Biol. 54, 441–454 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d51675e1234">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Cannon, S. B. et al. Polyploidy did not predate the evolution of nodulation in all legumes. PLoS ONE 5, e11630 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d51675e1237">32</a></sup> and associated <i>Ks</i> values in the cultivated alfalfa genome (~0.63) indicated an average mutation rate (<i>μ</i>) of 5.43 × 10<sup>−9</sup> per site per year in alfalfa, slightly lower than corresponding values in <i>M. truncatula</i> (<i>Ks</i> ~0.65, <i>μ</i> 5.60 × 10<sup>−9</sup> per site per year) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#Fig2">2c</a>).</p><p>TEs account for 55% of the assembled cultivated alfalfa genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">12</a> and Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM6">2</a>). Long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons are the most abundant TEs (account for 27.36% of the genome), and more abundant in the cultivated alfalfa genome than in <i>M. truncatula</i> (13.37%) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">12</a>). Among the LTR retrotransposons, the Ty3/Gypsy superfamily is more abundant than the Ty1/Copia superfamily in both cultivated alfalfa and <i>M. truncatula</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">12</a> and Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM6">2</a>). The comparison of genomic contents of cultivated alfalfa and <i>M. truncatula</i> shows that Ty3/Gypsy elements contribute most to the inflated cultivated alfalfa genome, accounting for 31.93% ((123 – 29 Mb)/295 Mb) of its genome size increment. Non-repeat sequences, Ty1/copia, simple/tandem repeat, and DNA element account for 26.57, 13.56, 9.93, and 6.87% of the total difference, respectively (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">12</a>). We also found that LTR bursts occurred in both species recently (&lt;2 Mya), after the two species diverged (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">11</a>), but stronger in the cultivated alfalfa. Collectively, these results show that accumulation of TE insertions was the main reason for the enlarged cultivated alfalfa’s genome.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Establishment of the CRISPR/Cas9 genome editing protocol</h3><p>The allele-aware chromosome-level cultivated alfalfa genome assembly obtained in this study provides a necessary start point to accurately apply the CRISPR/Cas9 technology to help in screening candidate genes, decoding gene structural information and designing optimal guide sequences (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#Fig3">3b</a>, detailed in “Methods”). Conversely, this genome editing technology could help efforts to convert the enormous amount of genome data into functionally relevant knowledge. A plant transformation binary vector named pMs-CRISPR/Cas9 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#Fig3">3a</a>) was constructed to stably transform alfalfa cultivars using <i>Agrobacterium tumefaciens</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">12</a>). In this vector, the CaMV 35S promoter is used to express <i>hSpCas9</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Feng, Z. et al. Efficient genome editing in plants using a CRISPR/Cas system. Cell Res. 23, 1229–1232 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d51675e1320">33</a></sup> and the selectable marker gene <i>Hygromycin phosphotransferase</i> (<i>Hpt</i>), and the MtU6 polymerase III promoter<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Meng, Y. et al. Targeted mutagenesis by CRISPR/Cas9 system in the model legume Medicago truncatula. Plant Cell Rep. 36, 371–374 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d51675e1331">34</a></sup> is used to drive expression of sgRNAs.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="CRISPR/Cas9-mediated genome editing in autotetraploid cultivated alfalfa."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: CRISPR/Cas9-mediated genome editing in autotetraploid cultivated alfalfa.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-16338-x/MediaObjects/41467_2020_16338_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-16338-x/MediaObjects/41467_2020_16338_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="537" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b> Schematic illustration of the Cas9 and sgRNA expression cassettes in the pMS-CRISPR/Cas9 vector (LB left border, RB right border, genes are represented by rectangles and promoters by arrows). The sgRNA consists of a guide sequence (blue rounded rectangle) and a scaffold, and the region between two AarI restriction endonuclease sites (light red) is used for the ligation of guide sequences. <b>b</b> Pipeline for designing guide sequences based on the cultivated alfalfa genome. <b>c</b> Guide sequence for <i>MsPDS</i>. Dark blue boxes and gray lines represent exons and introns, respectively. PAM is shown in red. <b>d</b>, <b>e</b> Genome editing of <i>MsPDS</i>. <b>d</b> Photos of three representative <i>MsPDS</i> mutants (<i>mspds</i>-1, <i>mspds</i>-4, and <i>mspds</i>-5), with <i>mspds</i>-1 exhibiting a wild type phenotype, and <i>mspds</i>-4 and <i>mspds</i>-5 exhibiting dwarf, albino phenotypes. Scale bar, 1 cm. <b>e</b> Sequencing of all screened mutants confirmed the presence of mutations at the target sites (light blue). The PAM regions are shown in black and lowercase. Nucleotide deletions, insertions or substitutions are shown in red, with details given in the right panel.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The <i>Phytoene desaturase</i> (<i>PDS</i>) gene was selected for the first test of this CRISPR/Cas9 system’s efficacy, as null <i>pds</i> mutants generally have clearly visible albino and dwarf phenotypes during juvenile stages<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Luo, M., Gilbert, B. &amp; Ayliffe, M. Applications of CRISPR/Cas9 technology for targeted mutagenesis, gene replacement and stacking of genes in higher plants. Plant Cell Rep. 35, 1439–1450 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d51675e1416">35</a></sup>. Four nearly identical <i>MsPDS</i> alleles were identified by analyzing the alfalfa genome assembly and manually checking (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">13</a>). A guide sequence located in the conserved region in exon 2 of <i>MsPDS</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#Fig3">3c</a>) was chosen, and was then synthesized and integrated into the pMs-CRISPR/Cas9 vector. After transformation, 50 plants were regenerated from 880 transformed calli, two of which (designated <i>mspds</i>-4 and <i>mspds</i>-5) exhibited the anticipated albino and dwarf phenotypes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#Fig3">3d</a>). All regenerated plants were initially screened for mutations by directly sequencing PCR amplicons encompassing the target site, and the mutagenesis frequency was defined as the number of mutants divided by the total number of transformed calli<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Zhang, Y. et al. Efficient and transgene-free genome editing in wheat through transient expression of CRISPR/Cas9 DNA or RNA. Nat. Commun. 7, 12617 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d51675e1443">18</a></sup>. Sequencing chromatograms indicated that five plants were mutants (5/880, 0.57%, designated as <i>mspds</i>1 to 5) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">14</a>). To further confirm the CRISPR/Cas9-induced <i>mspds</i> mutants and directly validate the sequencing results, PCR amplicons of <i>MsPDS</i> from candidate mutants were sub-cloned and 30 positive recombinant clones were randomly sequenced. This confirmed that all five screened mutants had mutated alleles at the target site. In detail, <i>mspds</i>-1, <i>mspds</i>-2, and <i>mspds</i>-3 contained three mutated alleles and one wild-type allele, while <i>mspds</i>-4 and <i>mspds</i>-5 had mutations in all four alleles (0.23%) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#Fig3">3e</a>). Due to the presence of a wild-type <i>MsPDS</i> allele, <i>mspds</i>-1, <i>mspds</i>-2, and <i>mspds</i>-3 plants displayed a wild type phenotype, whereas <i>mspds</i>-4 and <i>mspds</i>-5 plants displayed dwarf and albino phenotypes. The results of editing <i>MsPDS</i> demonstrate that the developed CRISPR/Cas9 system can be used for introducing mutations into the cultivated alfalfa genome. Importantly, null mutants can be created in the T0 generation.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Transgene-free and stably inherited mutations of <i>MsPALM1</i>
</h3><p>A high leaf/stem ratio is an important agronomic trait for cultivated alfalfa, as it is positively correlated to the nutritional value of alfalfa products<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Veronesi, F., Brummer, E. C. &amp; Huyghe, C. in Fodder Crops and Amenity Grasses. Vol. 5. (eds. Boller, B. et al.) 395–437 (Springer, New York, 2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d51675e1512">6</a></sup>. Breeding varieties with more leaflets per leaf may improve the leaf/stem ratio of cultivated alfalfa and thus increase its yield and nutritional value. In diploid <i>M. truncatula</i>, <i>PALM1</i> encodes a Cys(2)His(2) zinc finger transcription factor that plays a key role in compound leaf morphogenesis. Null <i>palm1</i> mutants develop palmate-like pentafoliate leaves rather than wild-type trifoliate leaves<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Chen, J. et al. Control of dissected leaf morphology by a Cys(2)His(2) zinc finger transcription factor in the model legume Medicago truncatula. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 107, 10754–10759 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d51675e1525">36</a></sup>. Thus, we hypothesized that disruption of <i>PALM1</i> orthologs (<i>MsPALM1</i>) in cultivated alfalfa may enable it to express the <i>palm1</i> phenotype. This would also provide another easily visible example to validate the stability of our protocol and its potential for generating multileaflet varieties. Four <i>MsPALM1</i> alleles were identified and all <i>MsPALM1</i> copies were found to have a single exon (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">15</a>). To disrupt <i>MsPALM1</i>, we selected a specific guide sequence to guide Cas9 to disrupt a BstUI restriction endonuclease site, thereby enabling easy screening of mutants through PCR-Restriction enzyme (PCR-RE) assay (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#Fig4">4a</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Genome editing of MsPALM1, and generating transgene-free and stably inherited palm1-type progenies."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Genome editing of <i>MsPALM1</i>, and generating transgene-free and stably inherited <i>palm1</i>-type progenies.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-16338-x/MediaObjects/41467_2020_16338_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-020-16338-x/MediaObjects/41467_2020_16338_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="720" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b> A guide sequence for <i>MsPALM1</i>. Dark blue box represents exon. PAM is shown in red. The BstUІ site is underlined and shown in light blue. <b>b</b> Leaf morphologies of three representative T0 plants. Scale bar, 1 cm. <b>c</b> Results of PCR-RE analyses for identifying mutants among T0 plants. In the gel, wt and wt-dg lanes contain DNA samples from wild-type plants without and with digestion by the BstUІ restriction endonuclease, respectively. Red arrowheads indicate bands used to identify mutations. Notably, <i>pa</i>T0<i>-</i>19 (highlighted with a red rectangle) yields dim digested bands (indicated by white arrows), although it develops palmate-like pentafoliate leaves (<b>b</b>). <b>d</b> Genotyping of the corresponding mutants in <b>b</b> confirmed the presence of mutations at the target sites (light blue). The PAM regions are shown in black and lowercase. Nucleotide deletions, insertions, or substitutions are shown in red, with details in the right panel. <b>e</b> Three representative T1 plants with two showing anticipated <i>palm1</i>-type leaf morphologies like their parents. Scale bar, 1 cm. <b>f</b>, <b>g</b> Results of PCR-RE analyses (<b>f</b>) and sequencing analyses (<b>g</b>) confirmed the parental <i>MsPALM1</i> mutations in corresponding T1 progenies in <b>e</b>. <b>h</b> Outcome of tests for transgene-free mutants in 20 corresponding T1 progenies in <b>f</b>. Lanes without bands (indicated by red arrowheads) identify transgene-free mutants. Lanes labeled wt, <i>pa</i>T0-19 and <i>pa</i>T0-46 show PCR fragments amplified from a WT plant, and two T0 mutants (<i>pa</i>T0-19 and <i>pa</i>T0-46), respectively. Source data underlying Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#Fig4">4c, f, h</a> are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In total, we identified 26 mutants from 1508 transformed calli (1.72%), including 12 <i>palm1</i>-type plants (0.80%) that developed palmate-like pentafoliate leaves (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#Fig4">4b, c</a>). Sanger sequencing of 20 clones of each mutant confirmed the presence of at least one mutated <i>MsPALM1</i> allele in their genomes, and all four alleles were disrupted in the <i>palm1</i>-type plants (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#Fig4">4d</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">16</a>). Notably, three <i>palm1</i>-type plants (<i>pa</i>T0-1, <i>pa</i>T0-19, and 29) were identified as chimeric mutants. Although the leaf morphology and sequencing results (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#Fig4">4b, d</a>) show that all four <i>MsPALM1</i> alleles in <i>pa</i>T0-19 were mutated, dim digested bands were still detected in PCR-RE analysis of this mutant (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#Fig4">4c</a>), indicating that wild-type alleles may persist in some of its cells. Furthermore, <i>pa</i>T0-1 and -29 contain up to five mutation types (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">16</a>), indicating that their cellular genotypes are not uniform, as reported in other T0 CRISPR/Cas9-edited plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Zhang, H. et al. The CRISPR/Cas9 system produces specific and homozygous targeted gene editing in rice in one generation. Plant Biotechnol. J. 12, 797–807 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d51675e1706">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Feng, Z. et al. Multigeneration analysis reveals the inheritance, specificity, and patterns of CRISPR/Cas-induced gene modifications in Arabidopsis. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 111, 4632–4637 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d51675e1709">38</a></sup>. To comprehensively investigate the off-target effects, whole genomes of three <i>palm1</i>-type mutants (<i>pa</i>T0-1, <i>pa</i>T0-19, and <i>pa</i>T0-46) were resequenced with 30-fold depth using IIlumina sequencing technology. Global scanning of these mutants’ whole genomes detected no off-target mutations in protein-coding regions besides the targeted regions (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">14</a>). This demonstrates that off-target effects of mutating cultivated alfalfa can be largely eliminated by using the developed CRISPR/Cas9-based genome editing protocol with the guidance from our high-quality genome.</p><p>Stable inheritance of agronomically beneficial mutations in cultivated alfalfa is hindered by its polyploidy and cross-pollination. To investigate whether the mutations and phenotypes of <i>palm1</i>-type mutants could be transmitted to the next generation, we harvested T1 seeds from <i>pa</i>T0-19 and <i>pa</i>T0-46 crosses (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">17</a>). Twenty seeds were randomly chosen and sown in a greenhouse, and 14 of the plants that germinated from them were <i>palm1</i>-type plants (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#Fig4">4e</a>). PCR-RE and sequencing analyses confirmed that each of these 14 <i>palm1</i>-type offspring contained four mutated <i>MsPALM1</i> alleles originating from their parents. Each of the six plants with wild-type phenotypes had at least one unmutated allele (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#Fig4">4f, g</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">18</a>), very possibly resulting from chimeric effects in <i>pa</i>T0-19. We also detected transgene-free plants by PCR analysis with two primers specific for <i>hSpCas9</i> and <i>Hpt</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">15</a>). The T-DNA fragment was found to be absent in 13 <i>palm1</i>-type progenies (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#Fig4">4h</a>). Collectively, these results show that our CRISPR/Cas9-based genome editing protocol can rapidly introduce heritable mutations and phenotypes into cultivated alfalfa in a transgene-free manner. In addition, the generation of these transgene-free <i>palm1-</i>type progenies indicates that CRISPR/Cas9 technology may provide a shortcut for breeding multileaflet varieties which may have higher nutritional value, although further studies are required to test whether the increase in leaflet number is accompanied by improvements in leaf biomass and forage quality.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><p>This study provides two complementary contributions, the chromosome-level reference genome and CRISPR/Cas9-based genome editing protocol, with substantial potential for accelerating fundamental investigation and breeding of cultivated alfalfa. In summary, by exploiting new sequencing technology and Hi-C scaffolding, we are able to decode the complex autotetraploid cultivated alfalfa genome, reveal events that have apparently shaped it, and create foundations for further studies on legumes and complex genome assembly. The genome is also a valuable resource for studies of alfalfa biology, evolution, and genome-wide mapping of QTLs associated with agronomically relevant traits. Due to its tetrasomy and self-incompatibility, improvement of cultivated alfalfa through traditional breeding approaches requires long breeding cycles and screening of extremely large populations in order to accumulate randomly occurring natural or mutagen-induced mutations conferring desirable traits at high frequencies. By contrast, using our genome assembly, we establish a reliable CRISPR/Cas9-based genome editing protocol for cultivated alfalfa that can precisely and simultaneously disrupt all alleles of selected genes (here, <i>MsPDS</i> and <i>MsPALM1</i>), thereby creating null mutants in a single generation. Most importantly, the mutated alleles and phenotypes can be stably transmitted to progenies by cross-pollination between two mutants in a transgene-free manner, which may help to accelerate the breeding speed and mitigate concerns about transgene technology and its products. The results also provide robust foundations for further technical developments, such as precise knock-in, base editing, or regulation of expression. Thus, they could potentially raise global food security by reducing breeding periods and costs of improving key agronomic traits of this important crop.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Sources and sequencing of genomic DNA/RNA</h3><p>Fresh leaves were plucked from a single cultivated alfalfa (cultivar XinJiangDaYe) plant cultivated in a greenhouse kept at 21–23 °C, 16 h light per day (light intensity of 380–450 W per m<sup>2</sup>) and a relative humidity (RH) of 70%. DNA was extracted from these leaves using a DNeasy Plant Mini Kit (Qiagen). Portions of the DNA were sent to AnnoRoad (Ningbo, China) to construct circular consensus sequencing (CCS) libraries and sequence them using a PacBio Sequal platform, and other portions were sent to Nextomics (Wuhan, China) to construct libraries and sequence them using Nanopore ONT and Illumina Hiseq platforms. These sequencing efforts yielded 70, 99, and 126 Gb of reads, respectively, for de novo assembly of the cultivated alfalfa genome (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">2</a>).</p><p>In addition, tender roots and shoots with leaves were collected, and RNA was extracted from one pooled root and shoot sample (with roughly the same weight of each organ) and four leaf samples using an RNeasy Plant Mini Kit (Qiagen). RNA samples were reverse-transcribed using random primers and sequenced using an Illumina platform. The RNA-seq data obtained are summarized in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">9</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Hi-C library construction and sequencing</h3><p>Fresh leaves and shoots were plucked from the plant used for whole genome sequencing, and then chromatin in the samples was cross-linked to DNA and fixed<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Wang, C. et al. Genome-wide analysis of local chromatin packing in Arabidopsis thaliana. Genome Res. 25, 246–256 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d51675e1834">39</a></sup>. Fixed samples were sent to BGI-Qingdao (Qingdao, China) for Hi-C library construction and sequencing. Two libraries were constructed using DpnII restriction endonuclease and 200 Gbp of data were obtained (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">3</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Genome size estimation</h3><p>Illumina data were cleaned using Trimmomatic (v. 0.36)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114–2120 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d51675e1849">40</a></sup> with default parameters. Two libraries (lib3 and lib4 in Supplementary Table 1), each with about 56 Gbp of reads, were used to estimate the cultivated alfalfa genome size by <i>K</i>-mer (<i>K</i> = 17) frequency-based methods with Kmerfreq in the SOAPec (v. 2.01) package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265–272 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d51675e1859">41</a></sup>. The estimated genome size was 1,578,294,649 bp, based on a frequency peak near 38× (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">1</a>), in accordance with previous findings<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Blondon, F., Marie, D., Brown, S. &amp; Kondorosi, A. Genome size and base composition in Medicago sativa and M. truncatula species. Genome 37, 264–270 (1994)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d51675e1867">42</a></sup>. Two other visible peaks near 20× and 70× reflect the heterozygosity associated with out-crossing and repetitive nature of auto-polyploid genomes. The heterozygosity rate is 3.7%, according to estimates obtained using a homemade script. The genome size of the sequenced individual was confirmed by flow cytometry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Blondon, F., Marie, D., Brown, S. &amp; Kondorosi, A. Genome size and base composition in Medicago sativa and M. truncatula species. Genome 37, 264–270 (1994)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d51675e1871">42</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">2</a>), as follows. Leaves from <i>M. sativa</i> (cultivar XinJiangDaYe) and <i>M. truncatula</i> (cultivar Jemalong, A17) plants were finely chopped together with a razor blade in 400 μl Galbraith buffer with 5 μl ml<sup>−1</sup> β-mercaptoethanol. The resulting suspension was filtered through 30-μm nylon. From a 500 U ml<sup>−1</sup> stock of Ribonuclease A, was added, from a 500 U ml<sup>−1</sup> stock solution, to 10 μl ml<sup>−1</sup> and propidium iodide to 50 μg ml<sup>−1</sup>. After 30 min incubation at room temperature, the DNA peak ratio was assessed by flow cytometry.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Genome assembly</h3><p>The cultivated alfalfa genome was assembled as follows: (1) We assembled contigs from CCS clean reads using Canu<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722–736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d51675e1903">22</a></sup>, with default parameters. The N50 values of the contig sets were 459 kb, with total lengths 3154 Mb. (2) Hi-C reads were aligned to contigs using HiC-Pro<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Servant, N. et al. HiC-Pro: an optimized and flexible pipeline for Hi-C data processing. Genome Biol. 16, 259 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d51675e1907">43</a></sup>, yielding an alignment BAM file. (3) Contigs were annotated with a solely homology-based strategy, using annotated <i>Medicago truncatula</i> proteins as references. 138,729 homologous genes were structurally annotated. MCscan in Jcvi (<a href="https://zenodo.org/record/31631#.XpkUyTOeask">https://zenodo.org/record/31631#.XpkUyTOeask</a>) was used to identify synteny blocks between contigs and the reference genome. Contigs syntenic to <i>M. truncatula</i> were stacked and aligned to <i>M. truncatula</i> chromosomes. The syntenic contigs are summarized in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">4</a>. (4) An in-house script was used to prune the BAM file and discard links between allelic contigs. Contigs syntenic to one chromosome of <i>M. truncatula</i>, e.g., chr1, were extracted, sub-clustered and reordered using ALLHiC<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Zhang, X., Zhang, S., Zhao, Q., Ming, R. &amp; Tang, H. Assembly of allele-aware, chromosomal-scale autopolyploid genomes based on Hi-C data. Nat. Plants 5, 833–845 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d51675e1934">44</a></sup>, yielding a raw scaffold set. (5) Juicebox<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Durand, N. C. et al. Juicebox provides a visualization system for Hi-C contact maps with unlimited zoom. Cell Syst. 3, 99–101 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d51675e1938">45</a></sup> was used for fine-tuning assembled scaffolds in a graphic and inter-active fashion. Forty scaffolds with a total length of 1800 Mb were cropped (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">5</a>). (6) Based on this scaffold assembly, each unplaced contig was assigned to the contig cluster, to which the contig was most connected by Hi-C data. (7) Those contig clusters were reordered and scaffolded using ALLHiC. (8) Using Juicebox, scaffolds were fine-tuned and discordant contigs were removed from scaffolds, and the final chromosome assembly was generated, containing 32 chromosomes with a total length of 2738 Mb (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">6</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Genome annotation</h3><p>Repetitive sequences of the cultivated alfalfa genome were annotated using both homology-based search and ab initio methods. TRF (v. 4.07b)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573–580 (1999)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d51675e1958">46</a></sup> was used to identify tandem repeats. RepeatProteinMask and RepeatMasker (v. 4.0.5) were utilized to search for known transposons (RepeatMasker using a library built by RepeatModeler) and LTR_FINDER was used for ab initio repeat identification (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">12</a>).</p><p>All repetitive regions except tandem repeats were soft-masked for protein-coding gene annotation. The coding sequences of <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>V. vinifera</i>, <i>G. max</i>, <i>Oryza sativa</i>, <i>M. truncatula</i>, <i>Cicer arietinum</i> and <i>Lotus japonicus</i> were downloaded. These coding sequences were subjected to Blast (v. 2.2.26) searches against the cultivated alfalfa genome and alignments were extracted for structural inspection by GeneWise in the Wise2 package (v. 2.2.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. GeneWise and genomewise. Genome Res. 14, 988–995 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d51675e1990">47</a></sup>. Homologs containing premature stop codons and frameshifts were discarded. Ms-root-shoot RNA-seq data were aligned to alfalfa contigs using Blat (v. 34)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Kent, W. J. BLAT-the BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656–664 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d51675e1994">48</a></sup> and GMAP (v. 2016-11-07)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Wu, T. D. &amp; Watanabe, C. K. GMAP: a genomic mapping and alignment program for mRNA and EST sequences. Bioinformatics 21, 1859–1875 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d51675e1998">49</a></sup>, and a comprehensive transcriptome database was built using PASA (v. 2.2.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 5654–5666 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d51675e2002">50</a></sup>. Open reading frames (ORFs) were predicted using TransDecoder, and the resulting database was used to train parameters for the following four de novo gene prediction software packages: AUGUSTUS (v. 3.2.2)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Stanke, M. &amp; Waack, S. Gene prediction with a hidden Markov model and a new intron submodel. Bioinformatics 19, ii215–ii225 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d51675e2007">51</a></sup>, GeneID (v. 1.4.4)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Blanco, E., Parra, G. &amp; Guigó, R. Using geneid to identify genes. Curr. Protoc. Bioinform. 18, 1–4 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d51675e2011">52</a></sup>, GlimmerHMM (v. 3.0.2)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Majoros, W. H., Pertea, M. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: two open source ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics 20, 2878–2879 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d51675e2015">53</a></sup>, and SNAP (v. 2006-07-28). Predictions obtained using these packages were then combined using EVM (v. 2012-06-25)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the program to assemble spliced alignments. Genome Biol. 9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d51675e2019">54</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">6</a>), then 87,479 protein-coding genes were retrieved and functionally annotated by blast searches against databases including UniProtKB/Swiss-Prot and UniProtKB/TrEMBL (last accessed on Sep 17th, 2014)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="UniProt, C. UniProt: a hub for protein information. Nucleic Acids Res. 43, D204–D212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d51675e2026">55</a></sup>. They were also subjected to GO annotation and protein family annotation by InterProScan (v. 5.17-56.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Zdobnov, E. M. &amp; Apweiler, R. InterProScan-an integration platform for the signature-recognition methods in InterPro. Bioinformatics 17, 847–848 (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d51675e2031">56</a></sup>. KO terms for each gene were assigned by blast searches against the KO database (last accessed on Sep 10th, 2014) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">11</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Genome synteny</h3><p>MCScanX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Tang, H. et al. Synteny and collinearity in plant genomes. Science 320, 486–488 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d51675e2046">57</a></sup> was employed to identify syntenic blocks in alfalfa and <i>M. truncatula</i>. Pairwise <i>Ks</i> values of syntenic paralogous genes were estimated by the “add-ka-and-ks-to-collinearity” program in MCscanX software, with Nei-Gojobori statistics. <i>Ks</i> values for each syntenic gene pair were then calculated with an in-house Perl script available at <a href="https://github.com/stanleyouth/-/blob/master/synteny_dn_ds.pl">https://github.com/stanleyouth/-/blob/master/synteny_dn_ds.pl</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Phylogenetic analysis</h3><p>All protein sequences from 11 species (<i>Arachis duranensis</i>, <i>Cajanus cajan</i>, <i>Cicer arietinum</i>, <i>G. max</i>, <i>Lotus japonica</i>, <i>Lupinus angustifolius</i>, <i>M. truncatula</i>, <i>P. vulgaris</i>, <i>T. pretense</i>, <i>Vigna angularis</i>, and <i>Vitis vinifera</i>) obtained from the NCBI database were used to generate clusters of gene families. As the allelic chromosomes have highly similar gene contents, the first allelic chromosome group (chr1.1–chr1.8) was selected to represent monoploid alfalfa. Gene sets were filtered by selecting the longest ORF for each gene. ORFs with premature stop codons, that were not multiples of three nucleotides long, or encoded less than 50 amino acids, were removed. Orthologous genes were identified by OrthoMCL. Single-copy genes (569) were identified, and subsequently used to build a phylogenetic tree. Coding DNA sequence (CDS) alignments of each single-copy family were created based on the protein alignment, using MUSCLE software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792–1797 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d51675e2109">58</a></sup>. A phylogenetic tree was reconstructed with RAxML software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics 30, 1312–1313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d51675e2113">59</a></sup> under the GTR+ gamma model with each single-copy gene and the concatenated sequence. ASTRAL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Zhang, C., Rabiee, M., Sayyari, E. &amp; Mirarab, S. ASTRAL-III: polynomial time species tree reconstruction from partially resolved gene trees. BMC Bioinform. 19, 153 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d51675e2117">60</a></sup> was used to construct a coalescent tree from the gene trees. We also extracted the most complete sequence for each BUSCO gene in each species, and then concatenated all the shared 980 single-copy BUSCO genes for tree building with the same method. Finally, the low copy gene-based (LCG) method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Waterhouse, R. M. et al. BUSCO applications from quality assessments to gene prediction and phylogenomics. Mol. Biol. Evol. 35, 543–548 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d51675e2121">61</a></sup> was applied to avoid the limitations of single copy genes, using a total of 5305 LCGs shared among the 12 species with less than ten copies in each species. The gene family trees were constructed and STAG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Emms, D. &amp; Kelly, S. STAG: species tree inference from all genes. bioRxiv. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1101/267914&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d51675e2125">62</a></sup> software was used to infer the species trees. To estimate divergence times, we used the PAML mcmctree program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586–1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d51675e2129">63</a></sup> for approximate likelihood calculations, with the single copy genes identified by OrthoMCL, a correlated molecular clock model and a REV substitution model. After a burn-in of 5,000,000 iterations, the MCMC process was repeated 20,000 times with a sample frequency of 5000. Convergence was checked by Tracer v. 1.4 (<a href="http://beast.community/tracer">http://beast.community/tracer</a>) and confirmed by two independent runs. Two constraints were applied in time calibrations: 105–115 Mya for the <i>V. vinifera</i>—leguminous split, and 49–62 Mya for the <i>Arachis duranensis</i>—other leguminous species split.</p><p>Target gene analysis and guide sequence design: To locate and clone candidate genes, homologs of query sequences (such as known CDSs of candidate genes or orthologs from other organisms) were sought by alignment with the alfalfa genome. After mapping whole genome sequencing (WGS) reads to the corresponding CDS, information of the candidate genes, such as copy number and gene structures, was deciphered and computational results were verified by experimental examination. Then, a series of guide sequences were extracted from selected genes using home-made scripts (<a href="https://github.com/stanleyouth/-/blob/master/crispr.sgRNA.finder.pl">https://github.com/stanleyouth/-/blob/master/crispr.sgRNA.finder.pl</a>). Guide sequences located in conserved coding exons were evaluated for potential off-target sites flanking protospacer adjacent motifs (PAMs: NGG and NAG) in the alfalfa genome using sgRNAcas9 (v. 3.0.5)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Xie, S., Shen, B., Zhang, C., Huang, X. &amp; Zhang, Y. sgRNAcas9: a software package for designing CRISPR sgRNA and evaluating potential off-target cleavage sites. PLoS ONE 9, e100448 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d51675e2157">64</a></sup>, which allows off-target sites with no more than 5 nt mismatches. Guide sequences were chosen that: covered all alleles; had no obvious off-target sites; were close to a start codon or in a functional conserved domain; had high GC content (which correlates with sgRNA efficacy); and started with a G at the 5′ end (required by the vector).</p><p>In this study, the mRNA sequence of the <i>MtPDS</i> gene of <i>M. truncatula</i> (accession code: <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/XM_024777859.1/">XM_024777859.1</a>) and CDS of the <i>MsPALM1</i> gene of <i>M. sativa</i> (accession code: <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/HM038483.1/">HM038483.1</a>) were used as query sequences to search and decode information on <i>MsPDS</i> and <i>MsPALM1</i>, respectively. Both <i>MsPDS</i> and <i>MsPALM1</i> were confirmed to have four alleles in the cultivated alfalfa genome. Series of guide sequences were extracted from exons of <i>MsPDS</i> and the single exon of <i>MsPALM1</i> (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM7">3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM8">4</a>). A previously used guide sequence in <i>M. truncatula</i> (5′-GCTGGAGGCAAGAGATGTTCT-3′)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Meng, Y. et al. Targeted mutagenesis by CRISPR/Cas9 system in the model legume Medicago truncatula. Plant Cell Rep. 36, 371–374 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d51675e2219">34</a></sup>, located in the conserved region in exon 2 of <i>MsPDS</i>, was inspected. It covers all <i>MsPDS</i> alleles and has no obvious off-target-site according to previous studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Kuscu, C., Arslan, S., Singh, R., Thorpe, J. &amp; Adli, M. Genome-wide analysis reveals characteristics of off-target sites bound by the Cas9 endonuclease. Nat. Biotechnol. 32, 677–683 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d51675e2230">65</a></sup> (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM7">3</a>). The best target (5′-GGAGACGAGCACGGTCGCGGCGG-3′), which contains a BstUI restriction endonuclease site that overlaps with the predicted cleavage site for the Cas9/sgRNA complex (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#Fig3">3c</a>), was screened for <i>MsPALM1</i>. No obvious off-target site was found for this guide sequence (Supplementary Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM8">4</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Construction of CRISPR/Cas9 binary vector</h3><p>The pMs-CRISPR/Cas9 vector was assembled by combining the expression cassettes of <i>hSpCas9</i> and sgRNA into the pCambia1300 entry vector, which contains a <i>Hpt</i> expression cassette. Pairs of oligos including guide sequences were synthesized as primers, annealed and cloned into AarI-digested pMs-CRISPR/Cas9 with T4 DNA ligase (NEB, Beijing, China). The pMs-CRISPR/Cas9 vector containing the guide sequence was transformed into competent <i>Escherichia coli</i> DH5α cells. Colony sequencing was used to confirm the correct insertion with Vt-F (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">15</a>). A single colony was then propagated by cultivation in liquid LB medium containing 50 mg l<sup>−1</sup> kanamycin, and the plasmid DNA was extracted using a TIANprep Mini Plasmid Kit (Tiangen, China) according to the manufacturer’s instructions. After that, plasmids of various CRISPR/Cas9 constructs were transferred into <i>Agrobacterium tumifaciens</i> strain EHA105 via electroporation for plant transformation experiments.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Plant materials, growth, and generation of transgenic plants</h3><p>Plants of the cultivated alfalfa cultivar Aohan (other cultivars were also used, unpublished data) were used as hosts for <i>Agrobacterium</i>-mediated transformation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Zhang, W. J. &amp; Wang, T. Enhanced salt tolerance of alfalfa (Medicago sativa) by rstB gene transformation. Plant Sci. 234, 110–118 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d51675e2283">66</a></sup> with some modification. Briefly, surface-sterilized seeds were sown on MS semi-solid medium and grown under long-day (16 h light/8 h dark) conditions at 25 °C. Fully developed cotyledonary explants from 7-day-old seedlings were excised and placed in Callus Induction Medium (SH basal salts and vitamins, 0.2 mg l<sup>−1</sup> kinetin, 2 mg l<sup>−1</sup> 2,4-D, 0.3 mg l<sup>−1</sup> casein hydrolysate, 30 g l<sup>−1</sup> sucrose, 8 g l<sup>−1</sup> agar, pH 5.8). <i>Agrobacterium tumefaciens</i> strain EHA105 carrying binary vector was used to transform calli, as follows. A suspension of the strain was prepared in MSH liquid medium containing 100 μM acetosyringone, 0.025 mg l<sup>−1</sup> kinetin and 2 mg l<sup>−1</sup> 2,4-D. Calli were then submerged in the suspension in a covered conical flask, placed on a shaker and rotated at 75 rpm at room temperature for 10 min. The calli were placed on sterilized filter paper in a Petri dish, then transferred to Co-incubation Medium (MS basal salts and vitamins, 2 mg l<sup>−1</sup> 2,4-D, 0.2 mg l<sup>−1</sup> kinetin, 100 μM acetosyringone, 30 g l<sup>−1</sup> sucrose, 8 g l<sup>−1</sup> agar, pH 5.8) in a growth chamber at 27 °C in the dark for 3 days. They were subsequently transferred onto Selection Medium (SH basal salts and vitamins, 0.2 mg l<sup>−1</sup> kinetin, 2 mg l<sup>−1</sup> 2,4-D, 250 mg l<sup>−1</sup> cefotaxime, 15 mg l<sup>−1</sup> hygromycin, 30 g l<sup>−1</sup> sucrose, 8 g l<sup>−1</sup> agar, pH 5.8) for 45 days. After selection cultivation, all calli were transferred to Shoot Induction Medium (SH basal salts and vitamins, 2 g l<sup>−1</sup> casein hydrolysate, 0.4 mg l<sup>−1</sup> kinetin, 250 mg l<sup>−1</sup> cefotaxime, 5 mg l<sup>−1</sup> hygromycin, 30 g l<sup>−1</sup> sucrose, 8 g l<sup>−1</sup> agar, pH 5.8) for more than 30 days, then regenerated shoots were transferred to Root Induction Medium (MS basal salts and vitamins, 250 mg l<sup>−1</sup> cefotaxime, 1 mg l<sup>−1</sup> IBA, 30 g l<sup>−1</sup> sucrose, 8 g l<sup>−1</sup> agar, pH 5.8). Finally, regenerated plants were transferred to soil and grown to maturity in a greenhouse.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Detecting mutations by PCR-RE assay and Sanger sequencing</h3><p>Genomic DNA was extracted from alfalfa leaf tissues using a DNA quick Plant System (Tiangen, China) according to the manufacturer’s instructions. Genomic regions surrounding the <i>MsPDS</i> and <i>MsPALM1</i> target sites were amplified by PCR with gene-specific primers (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">15</a>). For <i>MsPDS</i> gene, PCR products of individual plants were directly sequenced for screening mutants, and mutations were confirmed by sequencing 30 clones after cloning the PCR amplicons into the T vector pMD<sup>TM</sup> 19 (Takara, Japan). For the <i>MsPALM1</i> gene, PCR products were digested with BstUI restriction endonuclease (NEB, Beijing, China) according to the manufacturer’s instructions and the products were visualized by agarose gel electrophoresis. PCR amplicons of each mutant were cloned into the T vector pMD<sup>TM</sup> 19, and 20 clones were randomly picked for Sanger sequencing.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Analysis of off-target effects</h3><p>Three <i>palm1</i>-type mutants (<i>pa</i>T0-1, <i>pa</i>T0-19, and <i>pa</i>T0-46) were chosen for sequencing by an Illumina Hiseq 2500 platform, and a total of 97.4 Gb raw data were produced (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">13</a>). FastQC (<a href="https://www.bioinformatics.babraham.ac.uk/projects/fastqc/">https://www.bioinformatics.babraham.ac.uk/projects/fastqc/</a>) was used to evaluate the quality of the raw reads, then Trimmomatic (0.36)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114–2120 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d51675e2409">40</a></sup> was used, with default parameters, to exclude low quality reads. After that, the cleaned reads were mapped to the reference genome of cultivated alfalfa with Bwa (v. 0.7.12)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754–1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d51675e2413">67</a></sup>. Then the sam files were converted to bam files and duplicated reads were removed with Picard tools (v1.119, <a href="https://broadinstitute.github.io/picard/">https://broadinstitute.github.io/picard/</a>). Finally, the mutations were identified with suggested commands of the Genome Analysis Toolkit (GATK v. 3.5)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="McKenna, A. et al. The Genome Analysis Toolkit: a MapReduce framework for analyzing next-generation DNA sequencing data. Genome Res. 20, 1297–1303 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d51675e2424">68</a></sup>. After excluding the low quality mutations with suggested parameters, functional effects of the mutations were annotated with SnpEff<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Cingolani, P. et al. A program for annotating and predicting the effects of single nucleotide polymorphisms, SnpEff: SNPs in the genome of Drosophila melanogaster strain w1118; iso-2; iso-3. Fly 6, 80–92 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d51675e2428">69</a></sup>. These putative off-target mutations were manually examined to confirm whether they were indeed mutations and whether they were the targets themselves. According to previous report<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Peterson, B. A. et al. Genome-wide assessment of efficiency and specificity in CRISPR/Cas9 mediated multiple site targeting in Arabidopsis. PLoS ONE 11, e0162169 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d51675e2433">70</a></sup>, single nucleotide variations (SNVs) were excluded and only indel events at or near the −3 position relative to the PAM sequence were considered as Cas9-induced off-target mutations.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">PCR analysis for screening transgene-free progenies</h3><p>Genomic DNA was extracted from leaf tissues of T0 parents and T1 progenies as mentioned above, and then subjected to PCR using primers listed in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM1">15</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Downloaded data</h3><p><i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>V. vinifera</i>, <i>G. max</i>, <i>O. sativa</i>, and <i>M. truncatula</i> genome annotation files were downloaded from <a href="ftp://ftp.ensemblgenomes.org/pub/plants/release-39">ftp://ftp.ensemblgenomes.org/pub/plants/release-39</a>, <i>Cicer arietinum</i> genome and annotation files from <a href="ftp://parrot.genomics.cn/gigadb/pub/10.5524/100001_101000/100076">ftp://parrot.genomics.cn/gigadb/pub/10.5524/100001_101000/100076</a>, and <i>Lotus japonica</i> genome and annotation files from <a href="ftp://ftp.kazusa.or.jp/pub/lotus/lotus_r1.0">ftp://ftp.kazusa.or.jp/pub/lotus/lotus_r1.0</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Reporting summary</h3><p>Further information on research design is available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#MOESM3">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>Data supporting the findings of this work are available within the paper and its Supplementary Information files. A reporting summary for this article is available as a Supplementary Information file. The datasets generated and analyzed during the current study are available from the corresponding author upon request. All genome and transcriptome sequencing raw data described in this article are publicly available in the NCBI database under project <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/PRJNA540215">PRJNA540215</a>, and the genome assembly files are available at <a href="https://figshare.com/projects/whole_genome_sequencing_and_assembly_of_Medicago_sativa/66380">https://figshare.com/projects/whole_genome_sequencing_and_assembly_of_Medicago_sativa/66380</a>. The source data underlying Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#Fig1">1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-020-16338-x#Fig4">4c, f, h</a> are provided as a Source data file.</p>
            </div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="code-availability" data-title="Code availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="code-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="code-availability">Code availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="code-availability-content">
              
              <p>In-house perl scripts used in this study were deposited in Github, including scripts used for calculating <i>Ka</i>/<i>Ks</i> for synteny blocks [<a href="https://github.com/stanleyouth/-/blob/master/synteny_dn_ds.pl">https://github.com/stanleyouth/-/blob/master/synteny_dn_ds.pl</a>], and scripts used for sgRNA designing [<a href="https://github.com/stanleyouth/-/blob/master/crispr.sgRNA.finder.pl">https://github.com/stanleyouth/-/blob/master/crispr.sgRNA.finder.pl</a>].</p>

Zeng performed the off-target analysis; H.Z. and G.F. adapted Hi-C protocols for plant tissue and performed the Hi-C experiments; Q.Q. and W.W. supervised the project; H.C., Y. Zeng, Y.Y., Q.Q., and W.W. wrote the manuscript; J.C. and H.Y. helped in preparing and revising the manuscript.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding authors</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to

                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="FPar1">Competing interests</h3>
                <p>The strategies to apply CRISPR/Cas9 in genome editing of cultivated alfalfa and create <i>palm1</i>-type mutants as described in this paper have been filed for two Chinese patent applications (Application Number: 201810724589.8 and 201810724563.3) by Guangdong Sanjie Forage Biotechnology Co., Ltd. H.C. is an advisor to this company. All other authors declare no competing interests.</p>
